<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1946-10-08_21_Nazi_Chiefs_Guilty.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Oct 17, 1946 U.S. Newsreel of Nuremberg Trials Sentencing</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>20 November 1945: Start of the trials.</li>
<li>21 November 1945: Judge <a href="/wiki/Robert_H._Jackson" title="Robert H. Jackson">Robert H. Jackson</a> opens for the prosecution with a speech lasting several hours, leaving a deep impression on both the court and the public.</li>
<li>26 November 1945: The <a href="/wiki/Hossbach_Memorandum" title="Hossbach Memorandum">Hossbach Memorandum</a> (of a conference in which Hitler explained his war plans) is presented.</li>
<li>29 November 1945: The film "<a href="/wiki/Nazi_concentration_camps" title="Nazi concentration camps">Nazi concentration camps</a>" is screened.</li>
<li>30 November 1945: Witness <a href="/wiki/Erwin_von_Lahousen" title="Erwin von Lahousen">Erwin von Lahousen</a> testifies that Keitel and von Ribbentrop gave orders for the murder of Poles, Jews, and Russian <a href="/wiki/Prisoner_of_war" title="Prisoner of war">prisoners of war</a>.</li>
<li>11 December 1945: The film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Nazi_Plan" title="The Nazi Plan">The Nazi Plan</a></i> is screened, showing long-term planning and preparations for war by the Nazis.</li>
<li>3 January 1946: Witness <a href="/wiki/Otto_Ohlendorf" title="Otto Ohlendorf">Otto Ohlendorf</a>, former head of <i>Einsatzgruppe</i> D, detachedly admits to the murder of around 90,000 Jews.</li>
<li>3 January 1946: Witness <a href="/wiki/Dieter_Wisliceny" title="Dieter Wisliceny">Dieter Wisliceny</a> describes the organisation of <a href="/wiki/RSHA" class="mw-redirect" title="RSHA">RSHA</a> Department IV-B-4, in charge of the <a href="/wiki/Final_Solution" title="Final Solution">Final Solution</a>.</li>
<li>7 January 1946: Witness and former SS-<i>Obergruppenführer</i> <a href="/wiki/Erich_von_dem_Bach-Zelewski" title="Erich von dem Bach-Zelewski">Erich von dem Bach-Zelewski</a> admits to the organized mass murder of Jews and other groups in the Soviet Union.</li>
<li>28 January 1946: Witness <a href="/wiki/Marie-Claude_Vaillant-Couturier" title="Marie-Claude Vaillant-Couturier">Marie-Claude Vaillant-Couturier</a>, member of the French Resistance and concentration camp survivor, testifies on the Holocaust, becoming the first Holocaust survivor to do so.</li>
<li>11–12 February 1946: Witness and former Field Marshal <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Paulus" title="Friedrich Paulus">Friedrich Paulus</a>, who had been secretly brought to Nuremberg, testifies on the question of waging a war of aggression.</li>
<li>14 February 1946: The Soviet prosecutors try to blame the <a href="/wiki/Katyn_massacre" title="Katyn massacre">Katyn massacre</a> on the Germans.</li>
<li>19 February 1946: The film <i>Cruelties of the German-Fascist Intruders</i>, detailing the atrocities which took place in the <a href="/wiki/Extermination_camp" title="Extermination camp">extermination camps</a>, is screened.</li>
<li>27 February 1946: Witness <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Sutzkever" title="Abraham Sutzkever">Abraham Sutzkever</a> testifies on the murder of almost 80,000 Jews in Vilnius by the Germans occupying the city.</li>
<li>8 March 1946: The first witness for the defense testifies – former General <a href="/wiki/Karl_Bodenschatz" title="Karl Bodenschatz">Karl Bodenschatz</a>.</li>
<li>13–22 March 1946: <a href="/wiki/Hermann_G%C3%B6ring" title="Hermann Göring">Hermann Göring</a> takes the stand.</li>
<li>15 April 1946: Witness <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_H%C3%B6ss" title="Rudolf Höss">Rudolf Höss</a>, former commandant of Auschwitz, confirms that Kaltenbrunner had never been there, but admits to having carried out mass murder.</li>
<li>21 May 1946: Witness <a href="/wiki/Ernst_von_Weizs%C3%A4cker" title="Ernst von Weizsäcker">Ernst von Weizsäcker</a> explains the <a href="/wiki/German-Soviet_Non-Aggression_Pact" class="mw-redirect" title="German-Soviet Non-Aggression Pact">German-Soviet Non-Aggression Pact</a> of 1939, including its secret protocol detailing the division of Eastern Europe between Germany and the Soviet Union.</li>
<li>20 June 1946: <a href="/wiki/Albert_Speer" title="Albert Speer">Albert Speer</a> takes the stand. He is the only defendant to take personal responsibility for his actions.</li>
<li>29 June 1946: The defense for <a href="/wiki/Martin_Bormann" title="Martin Bormann">Martin Bormann</a> testifies.</li>
<li>1–2 July 1946: The court hears six witnesses testifying on the Katyn massacre; the Soviets fail to pin the blame for the event on Germany.</li>
<li>2 July 1946: Admiral <a href="/wiki/Chester_W._Nimitz" title="Chester W. Nimitz">Chester W. Nimitz</a> provides written testimony regarding attacks on merchant vessels without warning, admitting that Germany was not alone in these attacks, as the US did the same.</li>
<li>4 July 1946: Final statements for the defense.</li>
<li>26 July 1946: Final statements for the prosecution.</li>
<li>30 July 1946: Start of the trial of the "criminal organizations".</li>
<li>31 August 1946: Last statements by the defendants.</li>
<li>1 September 1946: The court adjourns.</li>
<li>30 September–1 October 1946: The sentencing occurs, taking two days, with the individual sentences read out on the afternoon of 1 October.<sup id="cite_ref-Catalogue10_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Catalogue10-67">[44]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>The accusers were successful in unveiling the background of developments leading to the outbreak of World War II, which cost at least 40 million lives in Europe alone,<sup id="cite_ref-google_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google-68">[45]</a></sup> as well as the extent of the atrocities committed in the name of the Hitler regime. Twelve of the accused were sentenced to death, seven received prison sentences (ranging from 10 years to life in prison), three were acquitted, and two were not charged.<sup id="cite_ref-countrystudies_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-countrystudies-69">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Executions">Executions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuremberg_trials&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Executions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_executions" title="Nuremberg executions">Nuremberg executions</a></div>
<p>The death sentences were carried out on 16 October 1946 by <a href="/wiki/Hanging" title="Hanging">hanging</a> using the <a href="/wiki/Hanging#Standard_drop" title="Hanging">standard drop method</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Hanging#Long_drop" title="Hanging">long drop</a>. The U.S. army denied claims that the drop length was too short which caused the condemned to die slowly from <a href="/wiki/Strangulation" class="mw-redirect" title="Strangulation">strangulation</a> instead of quickly from a broken neck,<sup id="cite_ref-time_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time-70">[47]</a></sup> but evidence remains that some of the condemned men died agonizingly slowly, struggling for 14 to 28 minutes before finally choking to death.<sup id="cite_ref-Judgment_at_Nuremberg_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Judgment_at_Nuremberg-71">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-All_Time.2C_part_two_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-All_Time.2C_part_two-72">[49]</a></sup> The executioner was <a href="/wiki/John_C._Woods" title="John C. Woods">John C. Woods</a>. Woods had hanged 34 U.S. soldiers during the war, botching several of them.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[50]</a></sup> The executions took place in the gymnasium of the court building (demolished in 1983).<sup id="cite_ref-Catalogue9_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Catalogue9-74">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although the rumor has long persisted that the bodies were taken to Dachau and burned there, they were actually incinerated in a crematorium in Munich, and the ashes scattered over the river <a href="/wiki/Isar" title="Isar">Isar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery2001205_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery2001205-75">[52]</a></sup> The French judges suggested that the military condemned (Göring, Keitel and Jodl) be shot by a <a href="/wiki/Firing_squad" class="mw-redirect" title="Firing squad">firing squad</a>, as is standard for military courts-martial, but this was opposed by Biddle and the Soviet judges, who argued that the military officers had violated their military ethos and were not worthy of death by being shot, which was considered to be more dignified.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The prisoners sentenced to incarceration were transferred to <a href="/wiki/Spandau_Prison" title="Spandau Prison">Spandau Prison</a> in 1947.</p>
<p>Of the 12 defendants sentenced to death by hanging, two were not hanged: <a href="/wiki/Martin_Bormann" title="Martin Bormann">Martin Bormann</a> was convicted in absentia (he had, unknown to the Allies, died while trying to escape from Berlin in May 1945), and Hermann Göring committed suicide the night before the execution. The remaining 10 defendants sentenced to death were hanged.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuremberg_principles">Nuremberg principles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuremberg_trials&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Nuremberg principles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The definition of what constitutes a war crime is described by the <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_principles" title="Nuremberg principles">Nuremberg principles</a>, a set of guidelines document which was created as a result of the trial. The medical experiments conducted by German doctors and prosecuted in the so-called <a href="/wiki/Doctors%27_Trial" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctors' Trial">Doctors' Trial</a> led to the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Code" title="Nuremberg Code">Nuremberg Code</a> to control future trials involving human subjects, a set of research ethics principles for human experimentation.</p>
<p>Of the indicted organizations the following were found not to be criminal:</p>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reichsregierung&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Reichsregierung (page does not exist)">Reichsregierung</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;(<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reichsregierung" class="extiw" title="de:Reichsregierung">de</a>)</span></i></li>
<li><i>"The General Staff and High Command" was found not to comprise a group or organization as defined by Article 9 of the <a href="/wiki/London_Charter" class="mw-redirect" title="London Charter">London Charter</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Sturmabteilung" title="Sturmabteilung">Sturmabteilung</a></i></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Subsidiary_and_related_trials">Subsidiary and related trials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuremberg_trials&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Subsidiary and related trials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The American authorities conducted <a href="/wiki/Subsequent_Nuremberg_Trials" class="mw-redirect" title="Subsequent Nuremberg Trials">subsequent Nuremberg Trials</a> in their occupied zone.</p>
<p>Other trials conducted after the Nuremberg Trials include the following:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Auschwitz_trial" title="Auschwitz trial">Auschwitz trial</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Belsen_trial" title="Belsen trial">Belsen trial</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Belzec_trial" title="Belzec trial">Belzec trial</a> before the 1st <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a> District Court in the mid-1960s, of eight SS men of the <a href="/wiki/Belzec_extermination_camp" class="mw-redirect" title="Belzec extermination camp">Belzec extermination camp</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Che%C5%82mno_trials" title="Chełmno trials">Chełmno trials</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Che%C5%82mno_extermination_camp" title="Chełmno extermination camp">Chełmno extermination camp</a> personnel, held in Poland and in Germany. The cases were decided almost twenty years apart</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dachau_trials" title="Dachau trials">Dachau trials</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Frankfurt_Auschwitz_trials" title="Frankfurt Auschwitz trials">Frankfurt Auschwitz trials</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Majdanek_trials" title="Majdanek trials">Majdanek trials</a>, the longest Nazi war crimes trial in history, spanning over 30 years</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mauthausen-Gusen_camp_trials" title="Mauthausen-Gusen camp trials">Mauthausen-Gusen camp trials</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hamburg_Ravensbr%C3%BCck_trials" title="Hamburg Ravensbrück trials">Hamburg Ravensbrück trials</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sobib%C3%B3r_trial" title="Sobibór trial">Sobibór trial</a> held in <a href="/wiki/Hagen" title="Hagen">Hagen</a>, Germany in 1965 against the SS men of the <a href="/wiki/Sobibor_extermination_camp" class="mw-redirect" title="Sobibor extermination camp">Sobibor extermination camp</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Treblinka_trials" title="Treblinka trials">Treblinka trials</a> in Düsseldorf, Germany</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="American_role_in_the_trial">American role in the trial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuremberg_trials&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: American role in the trial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jackson_Nuremberg_color.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Jackson_Nuremberg_color.jpg/220px-Jackson_Nuremberg_color.jpg" width="220" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Jackson_Nuremberg_color.jpg/330px-Jackson_Nuremberg_color.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Jackson_Nuremberg_color.jpg/440px-Jackson_Nuremberg_color.jpg 2x" data-file-width="978" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jackson_Nuremberg_color.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Chief American prosecutor Robert H. Jackson addresses the Nuremberg court. 20 November 1945.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While Sir Geoffrey Lawrence of Britain was the judge chosen as president of the court, the most prominent of the judges at trial arguably was his American counterpart, Francis Biddle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPersico2000111_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPersico2000111-76">[53]</a></sup> Prior to the trial, Biddle had been Attorney General of the United States but had been asked to resign by Truman earlier in 1945.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPersico200062_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPersico200062-77">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some accounts argue that Truman had appointed Biddle as the main American judge for the trial as an apology for asking for his resignation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPersico200062_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPersico200062-77">[54]</a></sup> Ironically, Biddle was known during his time as Attorney General for opposing the idea of prosecuting Nazi leaders for crimes committed before the beginning of the war, even sending out a memorandum on 5 January 1945 on the subject.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith197733_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith197733-78">[55]</a></sup> The note also expressed Biddle's opinion that instead of proceeding with the original plan for prosecuting entire organizations, there should simply be more trials that would prosecute specific offenders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith197733_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith197733-78">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>Biddle soon changed his mind, as he approved a modified version of the plan on 21 January 1945, likely due to time constraints, since the trial would be one of the main issues discussed at Yalta.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith197734_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith197734-79">[56]</a></sup> At trial, the Nuremberg tribunal ruled that any member of an organization convicted of war crimes, such as the SS or Gestapo, who had joined after 1939 would be considered a war criminal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPersico2000396_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPersico2000396-80">[57]</a></sup> Biddle managed to convince the other judges to make an exemption for any member who was drafted or had no knowledge of the crimes being committed by these organizations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPersico200062_77-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPersico200062-77">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Justice <a href="/wiki/Robert_H._Jackson" title="Robert H. Jackson">Robert H. Jackson</a> played an important role in not only the trial itself, but also in the creation of the International Military Tribunal, as he led the American delegation to London that, in the summer of 1945, argued in favour of prosecuting the Nazi leadership as a criminal conspiracy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeave197824_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeave197824-81">[58]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Airey_Neave#Wartime_service" title="Airey Neave">Airey Neave</a>, Jackson was also the one behind the prosecution's decision to include membership in any of the six criminal organizations in the indictments at the trial, though the IMT rejected this on the grounds that it was wholly without precedent in either international law or the domestic laws of any of the Allies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeave1978339.E2.80.9340_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeave1978339.E2.80.9340-82">[59]</a></sup> Jackson also attempted to have Alfried Krupp be tried in place of his father, Gustav, and even suggested that Alfried volunteer to be tried in his father's place.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeave1978297_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeave1978297-83">[60]</a></sup> Both proposals were rejected by the IMT, particularly by Lawrence and Biddle, and some sources indicate that this resulted in Jackson being viewed unfavourably by the latter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeave1978297_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeave1978297-83">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thomas Dodd was a prosecutor for the United States. There was an immense amount of evidence backing the prosecutors' case, especially since meticulous records of the Nazis' actions had been kept. There were records taken in by the prosecutors that had signatures from specific Nazis signing for everything from stationery supplies to <a href="/wiki/Zyklon_B_gas" class="mw-redirect" title="Zyklon B gas">Zyklon B gas</a>, which was used to kill the inmates of the deathcamps. Thomas Dodd showed a series of pictures to the courtroom after reading through the documents of crimes committed by the defendants. The showing consisted of pictures displaying the atrocities performed by the defendants. The pictures had been gathered when the inmates were liberated from the concentration camps.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Henry Gerecke, a Lutheran pastor, was sent to minister to the Nazi defendants.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuremberg_trials&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Tribunal is celebrated for establishing that "[c]rimes against international law are committed by men, not by abstract entities, and only by punishing individuals who commit such crimes can the provisions of international law be enforced."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeller2011.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3D8ALHAwFfy44C.26pg.3DPA3_3.5D_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeller2011.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3D8ALHAwFfy44C.26pg.3DPA3_3.5D-86">[63]</a></sup> The creation of the IMT was followed by trials of lesser Nazi officials and the trials of Nazi doctors, who performed experiments on people in prison camps. It served as the model for the <a href="/wiki/International_Military_Tribunal_for_the_Far_East" title="International Military Tribunal for the Far East">International Military Tribunal for the Far East</a> which tried Japanese officials for crimes against peace and against humanity. It also served as the model<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> for the <a href="/wiki/Eichmann_trial" class="mw-redirect" title="Eichmann trial">Eichmann trial</a> and for present-day courts at The Hague, for trying crimes committed during the Balkan wars of the early 1990s, and at Arusha, for trying the people responsible for the <a href="/wiki/Rwandan_genocide" title="Rwandan genocide">genocide</a> in Rwanda.</p>
<p>The Nuremberg trials had a great influence on the development of <a href="/wiki/International_criminal_law" title="International criminal law">international criminal law</a>. The Conclusions of the Nuremberg trials served as models for:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/The_Genocide_Convention" class="mw-redirect" title="The Genocide Convention">The Genocide Convention</a>, 1948.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a>, 1948.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Principles" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuremberg Principles">Nuremberg Principles</a>, 1950.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/The_Convention_on_the_Abolition_of_the_Statute_of_Limitations_on_War_Crimes_and_Crimes_against_Humanity" class="mw-redirect" title="The Convention on the Abolition of the Statute of Limitations on War Crimes and Crimes against Humanity">The Convention on the Abolition of the Statute of Limitations on War Crimes and Crimes against Humanity</a>, 1968.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Geneva_Convention" class="mw-redirect" title="Geneva Convention">Geneva Convention</a> on the Laws and Customs of War, 1949; its supplementary protocols, 1977.</li>
</ul>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Law_Commission" title="International Law Commission">International Law Commission</a>, acting on the request of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">United Nations General Assembly</a>, produced in 1950 the report <i>Principles of International Law Recognized in the Charter of the Nürnberg Tribunal and in the Judgement of the Tribunal</i> (Yearbook of the International Law Commission, 1950, vol. II<sup id="cite_ref-Yearbook_1950_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yearbook_1950-87">[64]</a></sup>). See <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Principles" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuremberg Principles">Nuremberg Principles</a>.</p>
<p>The influence of the tribunal can also be seen in the proposals for a permanent international criminal court, and the drafting of international criminal codes, later prepared by the International Law Commission.</p>
<p>Tourists can visit courtroom 600 on days when no trial is on. A permanent exhibition has been dedicated to the trials.<sup id="cite_ref-Nuremberg_museum_visitor_information_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nuremberg_museum_visitor_information-88">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Establishment_of_a_permanent_International_Criminal_Court">Establishment of a permanent International Criminal Court</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuremberg_trials&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Establishment of a permanent International Criminal Court">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Nuremberg trials initiated a movement for the prompt establishment of a permanent international criminal court, eventually leading over fifty years later to the adoption of the Statute of the <a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Court" title="International Criminal Court">International Criminal Court</a>. This movement was brought about because during the trials, there were conflicting court methods between the German court system and the U.S. court system. The crime of conspiracy was unheard of in the civil law systems of the Continent. Therefore, the German defense found it unfair to charge the defendants with conspiracy to commit crimes, while the judges from common-law countries were used to doing so.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[66]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (February 2017)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It [IMT] was the first successful international criminal court, and has since played a pivotal role in the development of international criminal law and international institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFichtelberg20095_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFichtelberg20095-90">[67]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuremberg_trials&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:45%; padding: 10px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 95%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: right; text-align: left;">
<div>You'll see. A few years from now the lawyers of the world will condemn this trial. You can't have a trial without law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilbert199536_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilbert199536-91">[68]</a></sup></div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: right; text-align: right;">&#160;—<a href="/wiki/Joachim_von_Ribbentrop" title="Joachim von Ribbentrop">Joachim von Ribbentrop</a><br />
20 November 1945</cite></div>
<p>Critics of the Nuremberg trials argued that the charges against the defendants were only defined as "crimes" after they were committed and that therefore the trial was invalid as a form of "<a href="/wiki/Victor%27s_justice" title="Victor's justice">victor's justice</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZolo2009_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZolo2009-92">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lecturesarchive_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lecturesarchive-93">[70]</a></sup> The alleged double standards associated with putative victor's justice are also evident from the indictment of German defendants for conspiracy to commit aggression against Poland in 1939, while no one from the Soviet Union was charged for being part of the same conspiracy. As Biddiss observed, "the Nuremberg Trial continues to haunt us.&#160;... It is a question also of the weaknesses and strengths of the proceedings themselves."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiddiss1995_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiddiss1995-94">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbc_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-95">[72]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-allacademic_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allacademic-96">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Quincy_Wright" title="Quincy Wright">Quincy Wright</a>, writing eighteen months after the conclusion of the IMT, explained the opposition to the Tribunal thus:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The assumptions underlying the Charter of the United Nations, the Statute of the International Court of Justice, and the Charter of the Nuremberg Tribunal are far removed from the positivistic assumptions which greatly influenced the thought of international jurists in the nineteenth century. Consequently, the activities of those institutions have frequently been vigorously criticized by positivistic jurists&#160;... [who] have asked: How can principles enunciated by the Nuremberg Tribunal, to take it as an example, be of legal value until most of the states have agreed to a tribunal with jurisdiction to enforce those principles? How could the Nuremberg Tribunal have obtained jurisdiction to find Germany guilty of aggression, when Germany had not consented to the Tribunal? How could the law, first explicitly accepted in the Nuremberg Charter of 1945, have bound the defendants in the trial when they committed the acts for which they were indicted years earlier?<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright1948405.E2.80.937_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright1948405.E2.80.937-97">[74]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; padding: 10px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>Art.19 "The Tribunal shall not be bound by technical rules of evidence."</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">Charter of the International Military Tribunal</cite></div>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; padding: 10px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>Art.21 "The Tribunal shall not require proof of facts of common knowledge but shall take judicial notice thereof."</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">Charter of the International Military Tribunal</cite></div>
<p><br />
<br /></p>
<p>Chief Justice of the United States Supreme Court <a href="/wiki/Harlan_Fiske_Stone" class="mw-redirect" title="Harlan Fiske Stone">Harlan Fiske Stone</a> called the Nuremberg trials a fraud.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMason1968716_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMason1968716-98">[75]</a></sup> "(Chief U.S. prosecutor) Jackson is away conducting his high-grade lynching party in Nuremberg," he wrote. "I don't mind what he does to the Nazis, but I hate to see the pretense that he is running a court and proceeding according to common law. This is a little too sanctimonious a fraud to meet my old-fashioned ideas."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMason1968716_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMason1968716-98">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson, in a letter discussing the weaknesses of the trial, in October 1945 told U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a> that the Allies themselves "have done or are doing some of the very things we are prosecuting the Germans for. The French are so violating the Geneva Convention in the treatment of prisoners of war that our command is taking back prisoners sent to them. We are prosecuting plunder and our Allies are practising it. We say aggressive war is a crime and one of our allies asserts sovereignty over the Baltic States based on no title except conquest."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELuban1994.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DNvBiDFPApOkC.26pg.3DPA360_360.E2.80.931.5D_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELuban1994.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DNvBiDFPApOkC.26pg.3DPA360_360.E2.80.931.5D-99">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Legacy_of_Nuremberg_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Legacy_of_Nuremberg-100">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>Associate Supreme Court Justice <a href="/wiki/William_O._Douglas" title="William O. Douglas">William O. Douglas</a> charged that the Allies were guilty of "substituting power for principle" at Nuremberg. "I thought at the time and still think that the Nuremberg trials were unprincipled," he wrote. "Law was created <i>ex post facto</i> to suit the passion and clamor of the time."<sup id="cite_ref-reappraisal_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reappraisal-101">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>U.S. Deputy Chief Counsel <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Pomerantz" title="Abraham Pomerantz">Abraham Pomerantz</a> resigned in protest at the low caliber of the judges assigned to try the industrial war criminals such as those at <a href="/wiki/I.G._Farben" class="mw-redirect" title="I.G. Farben">I.G. Farben</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Treason.27s_Peace_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Treason.27s_Peace-102">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>A number of Germans who agreed with the idea of punishment for war crimes admitted trepidation concerning the trials. A contemporary German jurist said:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>That the defendants at Nuremberg were held responsible, condemned and punished, will seem to most of us initially as a kind of historical justice. However, no one who takes the question of guilt seriously, above all no responsibly thoughtful jurist, will be content with this sensibility nor should they be allowed to be. Justice is not served when the guilty parties are punished in any old way, even if this seems appropriate with regard to their measure of guilt. Justice is only served when the guilty are punished in a way that carefully and conscientiously considers their criminal errors according to the provisions of valid law under the jurisdiction of a legally appointed judge.<sup id="cite_ref-cambridge_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cambridge-103">[80]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The validity of the court has been questioned on a number of grounds</p>
<ul>
<li>The trials were conducted under their own <a href="/wiki/Rules_of_evidence" class="mw-redirect" title="Rules of evidence">rules of evidence</a>. The Charter of the International Military Tribunal permitted the use of normally inadmissible "evidence". Article 19 specified that "The Tribunal shall not be bound by technical rules of evidence&#160;... and shall admit any evidence which it deems to have probative value". Article 21 of the Nuremberg International Military Tribunal (IMT) Charter stipulated:</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd>The Tribunal shall not require proof of facts of common knowledge but shall take <a href="/wiki/Judicial_notice" title="Judicial notice">judicial notice</a> thereof. It shall also take judicial notice of official governmental documents and reports of the United [Allied] Nations, including acts and documents of the committees set up in the various allied countries for the investigation of war crimes, and the records and findings of military and other Tribunals of any of the United [Allied] Nations.</dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>The defendants were not allowed to appeal or affect the selection of judges.
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/A._L._Goodhart" class="mw-redirect" title="A. L. Goodhart">A. L. Goodhart</a>, Professor at <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxford University">Oxford</a>, opposed the view that, because the judges were appointed by the victors, the Tribunal was not impartial and could not be regarded as a court in the true sense. He wrote: Attractive as this argument may sound in theory, it ignores the fact that it runs counter to the administration of law in every country. If it were true then no spy could be given a legal trial, because his case is always heard by judges representing the enemy country. Yet no one has ever argued that in such cases it was necessary to call on neutral judges. The prisoner has the right to demand that his judges shall be fair, but not that they shall be neutral. As Lord Writ has pointed out, the same principle is applicable to ordinary criminal law because 'a burglar cannot complain that he is being tried by a jury of honest citizens'.<sup id="cite_ref-nuremberg7_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nuremberg7-104">[81]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>One of the charges, brought against Keitel, Jodl, and Ribbentrop included conspiracy to commit aggression against Poland in 1939. The Secret Protocols of the <a href="/wiki/German-Soviet_Non-Aggression_Pact" class="mw-redirect" title="German-Soviet Non-Aggression Pact">German-Soviet Non-Aggression Pact</a> of 23 August 1939, proposed the partition of Poland between the Germans and the Soviets (which was subsequently executed in September 1939); however, Soviet leaders were not tried for being part of the same conspiracy.<sup id="cite_ref-encyclopedia_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-encyclopedia-105">[82]</a></sup> Instead, the Tribunal proclaimed the Secret Protocols of the Non-Aggression Pact to be a forgery. Moreover, Allied Powers Britain and Soviet Union were not tried for preparing and conducting the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Soviet_invasion_of_Iran" title="Anglo-Soviet invasion of Iran">Anglo-Soviet invasion of Iran</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Winter_War" title="Winter War">Winter War</a>, respectively.</li>
<li>In 1915, the Allied Powers, Britain, France, and Russia, jointly issued a statement explicitly charging, for the first time, another government (the <a href="/wiki/Sublime_Porte" title="Sublime Porte">Sublime Porte</a>) of committing "a <a href="/wiki/Crime_against_humanity" class="mw-redirect" title="Crime against humanity">crime against humanity</a>". However it was not until the phrase was further developed in the <i>London Charter</i> that it had a specific meaning. As the London Charter definition of what constituted a crime against humanity was unknown when many of the crimes were committed, it could be argued to be a retroactive law, in violation of the principles of prohibition of <a href="/wiki/Ex_post_facto_law" title="Ex post facto law">ex post facto laws</a> and the general principle of penal law <i><a href="/wiki/Nullum_crimen,_nulla_poena_sine_praevia_lege_poenali" class="mw-redirect" title="Nullum crimen, nulla poena sine praevia lege poenali">nullum crimen, nulla poena sine praevia lege poenali</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Motion_adopted_by_all_defense_counsel_group.3Davalon_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Motion_adopted_by_all_defense_counsel_group.3Davalon-106">[avalon 24]</a></sup></li>
<li>The court agreed to relieve the Soviet leadership from attending these trials as war criminals in order to hide their <a href="/wiki/Soviet_war_crimes" title="Soviet war crimes">crimes against war civilians</a>, war crimes that were committed by their army that included "carving up Poland in 1939 and attacking Finland three months later." This "exclusion request" was initiated by the Soviets and subsequently approved by the court's administration.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc8_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc8-107">[83]</a></sup></li>
<li>Though the <a href="/wiki/ICTY" class="mw-redirect" title="ICTY">ICTY</a> later held it to be flawed in principle",<sup id="cite_ref-Lee_2004_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lee_2004-108">[84]</a></sup> the <i><a href="/wiki/Tu_quoque" title="Tu quoque">tu quoque</a></i> argument, adduced by German defendants, was admitted as a valid defense during the trials, and the admirals Dönitz and Raeder were not punished for waging unrestricted submarine warfare.<sup id="cite_ref-Lee_2004_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lee_2004-108">[84]</a></sup></li>
<li>The chief Soviet prosecutor submitted false documentation in an attempt to indict defendants for the murder of thousands of Polish officers in the Katyn forest near Smolensk. However, the other Allied prosecutors refused to support the indictment and German lawyers promised to mount an embarrassing defense. No one was charged or found guilty at Nuremberg for the <a href="/wiki/Katyn_Massacre" class="mw-redirect" title="Katyn Massacre">Katyn Forest massacre</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-German_Clobbers_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-German_Clobbers-109">[85]</a></sup> In 1990, the Soviet government acknowledged that the Katyn massacre was carried out, not by the Germans, but by the Soviet secret police.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc161204_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc161204-110">[86]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (April 2017)">original research?</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Freda_Utley" title="Freda Utley">Freda Utley</a>, in her 1949 book <i>"The High Cost of Vengeance"</i><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[87]</a></sup> charged the court with amongst other things double standards. She pointed to the Allied use of <a href="/wiki/Forced_labor_of_Germans_in_the_Soviet_Union" title="Forced labor of Germans in the Soviet Union">civilian forced labor</a>, and deliberate starvation of civilians<sup id="cite_ref-austincollege_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-austincollege-112">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson1999.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DkEAFk4lce4kC.26pg.3DPA85_85.5D._US_military_personnel_and_their_wives_were_under_strict_orders_to_destroy_or_otherwise_render_inedible_their_own_leftover_surplus_so_as_to_ensure_it_could_not_be_eaten_by_German_civilians_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson1999.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DkEAFk4lce4kC.26pg.3DPA85_85.5D._US_military_personnel_and_their_wives_were_under_strict_orders_to_destroy_or_otherwise_render_inedible_their_own_leftover_surplus_so_as_to_ensure_it_could_not_be_eaten_by_German_civilians-113">[89]</a></sup> in the occupied territories. She also noted that General Rudenko, the chief Soviet prosecutor, after the trials became commandant of the <a href="/wiki/Sachsenhausen_concentration_camp" title="Sachsenhausen concentration camp">Sachsenhausen concentration camp</a>. (After the fall of East Germany the bodies of 12,500 Soviet era victims were uncovered at the camp, mainly "children, adolescents and elderly people."<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes-114">[90]</a></sup>)</li>
<li>Luise, the wife of <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Jodl" title="Alfred Jodl">Alfred Jodl</a>, attached herself to her husband's defense team. Subsequently, interviewed by <a href="/wiki/Gitta_Sereny" title="Gitta Sereny">Gitta Sereny</a>, researching her biography of <a href="/wiki/Albert_Speer" title="Albert Speer">Albert Speer</a>, Luise alleged that in many instances the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allied</a> prosecution made charges against Jodl based on documents that they refused to share with the defense. Jodl nevertheless proved some of the charges made against him were untrue, such as the charge that he helped Hitler gain control of Germany in 1933. He was in one instance aided by a <a href="/wiki/GI_(term)" class="mw-redirect" title="GI (term)">GI</a> clerk who chose to give Luise a document showing that the execution of a group of British commandos in <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> had been legitimate. The GI warned Luise that if she didn't copy it immediately she would never see it again.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESereny1995.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3D_Z3gKDdRgL8C.26pg.3DPA578_578.5D_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESereny1995.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3D_Z3gKDdRgL8C.26pg.3DPA578_578.5D-115">[91]</a></sup></li>
<li>The main Soviet judge, <a href="/wiki/Iona_Nikitchenko" title="Iona Nikitchenko">Iona Nikitchenko</a>, presided over some of the most notorious of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Show_trial" title="Show trial">show trials</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Purge" title="Great Purge">Great Purges</a> of 1936 to 1938, where he among other things sentenced <a href="/wiki/Lev_Kamenev" title="Lev Kamenev">Kamenev</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grigory_Zinoviev" title="Grigory Zinoviev">Zinoviev</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-yandex_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yandex-116">[92]</a></sup> According to the declassified Soviet archives, 681,692 people arrested for "counter-revolutionary and state crimes" were shot in 1937 and 1938 alone–an average of over 900 executions a day.<sup id="cite_ref-google9_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google9-117">[93]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Soviet prosecutor, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Rudenko" title="Roman Rudenko">Roman Rudenko</a>, later became commandant of <a href="/wiki/NKVD_special_camp_Nr._7" title="NKVD special camp Nr. 7">NKVD special camp Nr. 7</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Parish_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parish-118">[94]</a></sup> By the time the camp closed in the spring of 1950, at least 12,000 prisoners had died due to the catastrophic prison conditions, hunger and psychological or physical exhaustion.<sup id="cite_ref-shiftung_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shiftung-119">[95]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Tribunal itself strongly disputed that the <a href="/wiki/London_Charter_of_the_International_Military_Tribunal" class="mw-redirect" title="London Charter of the International Military Tribunal">London Charter</a> was <i>ex post facto</i> law, pointing to existing international agreements signed by Germany that made aggressive war and certain wartime actions unlawful, such as the <a href="/wiki/Kellogg-Briand_Pact" class="mw-redirect" title="Kellogg-Briand Pact">Kellogg-Briand Pact</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Covenant_of_the_League_of_Nations" title="Covenant of the League of Nations">Covenant of the League of Nations</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Hague_Conventions_of_1899_and_1907" title="Hague Conventions of 1899 and 1907">Hague Conventions of 1899 and 1907</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IMT_Judgement_group.3Davalon_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IMT_Judgement_group.3Davalon-120">[avalon 25]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>In an editorial at the time <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i>, a British weekly newspaper, criticised the hypocrisy of both Britain and France for supporting the expulsion of the Soviet Union from the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> over its unprovoked attack against Finland in 1939 and for six years later cooperating with the USSR as a respected equal at Nuremberg. It also criticised the allies for their own double-standard at the Nuremberg Trials: "nor should the Western world console itself that the Russians alone stand condemned at the bar of the Allies' own justice.&#160;... Among crimes against humanity stands the offence of the indiscriminate bombing of civilian populations. Can the Americans who dropped the atom bomb and the British who destroyed the cities of western Germany plead 'not guilty' on this count? Crimes against humanity also include the mass expulsion of populations. Can the Anglo-Saxon leaders who at Potsdam condoned the expulsion of millions of Germans from their homes hold themselves completely innocent?&#160;... The nations sitting in judgement have so clearly proclaimed themselves exempt from the law which they have administered."<sup id="cite_ref-nuremberg10_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nuremberg10-121">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legitimacy">Legitimacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuremberg_trials&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Legitimacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Maxwell_Fyfe_and_another.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/75/David_Maxwell_Fyfe_and_another.jpg/220px-David_Maxwell_Fyfe_and_another.jpg" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/75/David_Maxwell_Fyfe_and_another.jpg/330px-David_Maxwell_Fyfe_and_another.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/75/David_Maxwell_Fyfe_and_another.jpg/440px-David_Maxwell_Fyfe_and_another.jpg 2x" data-file-width="596" data-file-height="459" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Maxwell_Fyfe_and_another.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/David_Maxwell_Fyfe,_1st_Earl_of_Kilmuir" title="David Maxwell Fyfe, 1st Earl of Kilmuir">Sir David Maxwell Fyfe</a> (at lectern, left) and an unknown <a href="/wiki/Prosecutor" title="Prosecutor">prosecutor</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>One criticism that was made of the IMT was that some treaties were not binding on the Axis powers because they were not signatories. This was addressed in the judgment relating to war crimes and crimes against humanity,<sup id="cite_ref-yale_group.3Davalon_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yale_group.3Davalon-122">[avalon 26]</a></sup> which contains an expansion of customary law: "<a href="/wiki/Hague_Convention_of_1907" class="mw-redirect" title="Hague Convention of 1907">the [Hague] Convention</a> expressly stated that it was an attempt 'to revise the general laws and customs of war,' which it thus recognised to be then existing, but by 1939 these rules laid down in the Convention were recognised by all civilised nations, and were regarded as being declaratory of the laws and customs of war which are referred to in Article 6 (b) of the [London] Charter."</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction_of_extempore_simultaneous_interpretation">Introduction of extempore simultaneous interpretation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuremberg_trials&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Introduction of extempore simultaneous interpretation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nuremberg_trials&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <small><i>(July 2017)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The Nuremberg Trials employed four <a href="/wiki/Official_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Official languages">official languages</a>: <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>. In order to address the complex linguistic issues that clouded over the proceedings, <a href="/wiki/Language_interpretation" title="Language interpretation">interpretation</a> and translation departments had to be established. However, it was feared that consecutive interpretation would slow down the proceedings significantly. What is therefore unique in both the Nuremberg tribunals and history of the interpretation profession was the introduction of an entirely new technique, extempore simultaneous interpretation. This technique of interpretation requires the interpreter to listen to a speaker in a source (or passive) language and orally translate that speech into another language in real time, that is, simultaneously, through headsets and microphones. Interpreters were split into four sections, one for each official language, with three interpreters per section working from the other three languages into the fourth (their mother tongue). For instance, the English booth consisted of three interpreters, one working from German into English, one working from French, and one from Russian, etc. Defendants who did not speak any of the four official languages were provided with consecutive court interpreters. Some of the languages heard over the course of the proceedings included <a href="/wiki/Yiddish" title="Yiddish">Yiddish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">Czech</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_language" title="Ukrainian language">Ukrainian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a>.</p>
<p>The equipment used to establish this system was provided by <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a>, and included an elaborate setup of cables which were hooked up to headsets and single earphones directly from the four interpreting booths (often referred to as "the aquarium"). Four channels existed for each working language, as well as a root channel for the proceedings without interpretation. Switching of channels was controlled by a setup at each table in which the listener merely had to turn a dial in order to switch between languages. People tripping over the floor-laid cables often led to the headsets getting disconnected, with several hours at a time sometimes being taken in order to repair the problem and continue on with the trials.</p>
<p>Interpreters were recruited and examined by the respective countries in which the official languages were spoken: US, UK, France, the Soviet Union, Germany, <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, as well as in special cases <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>. Many were former <a href="/wiki/Translators" class="mw-redirect" title="Translators">translators</a>, army personnel, and <a href="/wiki/Linguists" class="mw-redirect" title="Linguists">linguists</a>, some were experienced consecutive interpreters, others were ordinary individuals and even recent secondary school-graduates who led international lives in multilingual environments. It was, and still is believed, that the qualities that made the best interpreters were not just a perfect understanding of two or more languages, but more importantly a broad sense of culture, encyclopædic knowledge, inquisitiveness, as well as a naturally calm disposition.</p>
<p>With the simultaneous technique being extremely new, interpreters practically trained themselves, but many could not handle the pressure or the psychological strain. Many often had to be replaced, many returned to the translation department, and many left. Serious doubts were given as to whether interpretation provided a fair trial for the defendants, particularly because of fears of mistranslation and errors made on transcripts. The translation department had to also deal with the overwhelming problem of being understaffed and overburdened with an influx of documents that could not be kept up with. More often than not, interpreters were stuck in a session without having proper documents in front of them and were relied upon to do sight translation or double translation of texts, causing further problems and extensive criticism. Other problems that arose included complaints from lawyers and other legal professionals with regard to <a href="/wiki/Interrogation" title="Interrogation">questioning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cross-examination" title="Cross-examination">cross-examination</a>. Legal professionals were most often appalled at the slower speed at which they had to conduct their task because of the extended time required for interpreters to do an interpretation properly. Also, a number of interpreters were noted for protesting the idea of using vulgar language reflected in the proceeds, especially if it referenced <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> or the conditions of the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_concentration_camps" title="Nazi concentration camps">Nazi concentration camps</a>. Bilingual/trilingual members who attended the trials picked up quickly on this aspect of character and were equally quick to file complaints.</p>
<p>Yet, despite the extensive trial and error, without the interpretation system the trials would not have been possible and in turn revolutionized the way multilingual issues were addressed in tribunals and conferences. A number of the interpreters following the trials were immediately recruited into the newly formed United Nations, while others returned to their ordinary lives, pursued other careers, or worked freelance. Outside the boundaries of the trials, many interpreters continued their positions on weekends interpreting for dinners, private meetings between judges, and excursions between delegates. Others worked as investigators or editors, or aided the translation department when they could, often using it as an opportunity to sharpen their skills and to correct poor interpretations on transcripts before they were available for public record.</p>
<p>For further reference, a book titled <i>The Origins of Simultaneous Interpretation: The Nuremberg Trial</i>, written by interpreter Francesca Gaiba, was published by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Ottawa_Press" title="University of Ottawa Press">University of Ottawa Press</a> in 1998.</p>
<p>Today, all major international organizations, as well as any conference or government that uses more than one <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a>, uses extempore simultaneous interpretation. Notable bodies include the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Kosovo" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliament of Kosovo">Parliament of Kosovo</a> with three official languages, the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Canada" title="Parliament of Canada">Parliament of Canada</a> with two official languages, the Parliament of South Africa with eleven official languages, the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> with twenty-four official languages, and the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> with six official working languages.</p>

According to <a href="#CITEREFMarrus1997">Marrus 1997</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/stable/41212687">page 563</a>, Roosevelt had already written to Rabbi Stephen Wise, president of the American Jewish Congress, in July 1942, saying: "The American people not only sympathise with all victims of Nazi crimes, but will hold the perpetrators of these crimes to strict accountability in a day of reckoning which will surely come."</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-From_Nuremberg_to_the_Hague-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-From_Nuremberg_to_the_Hague_47-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Clapham, Andrew (2003). "Issues of complexity, complicity and complementarity: from the Nuremberg Trials to the dawn of the International Criminal". In Philippe Sands. <i>From Nuremberg to the Hague: the future of international criminal justice</i>. Cambridge University Press. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-521-82991-7" title="Special:BookSources/0-521-82991-7">0-521-82991-7</a>. <q>The tribunal's eventual decision was that Gustav Krupp could not be tried because of his condition but that 'the charges against him in the Indictment should be retained for trial thereafter if the physical and mental condition of the defendant should permit'.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANuremberg+trials&amp;rft.atitle=Issues+of+complexity%2C+complicity+and+complementarity%3A+from+the+Nuremberg+Trials+to+the+dawn+of+the+International+Criminal&amp;rft.aufirst=Andrew&amp;rft.aulast=Clapham&amp;rft.btitle=From+Nuremberg+to+the+Hague%3A+the+future+of+international+criminal+justice&amp;rft.date=2003&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.isbn=0-521-82991-7&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEBower1995347-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBower1995347_55-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBower1995">Bower 1995</a>, p.&#160;347.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-56"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-56">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">William L Shierer "the Rise and Fall of the third Reich", part IV, Nuremberg-chapter</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrunner2001234-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrunner2001234_61-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBrunner2001">Brunner 2001</a>, p.&#160;234.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFGilbert1995">Gilbert 1995</a>, pp.&#160;30–1. Gilbert does not provide Ley's IQ, presumably because Ley committed suicide before the tests were done, though Gilbert is not explicit on this point. <a href="#CITEREFSereny1995">Sereny 1995</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=_Z3gKDdRgL8C&amp;pg=PA573">p. 573</a>, records that Speer regarded the tests as "idiotic", and so responded to them, and to the Rorschach test in particular, with "total nonsense". Coincidentally, <a href="#CITEREFBrunner2001">Brunner 2001</a>, p. 234, argues that, given the circumstances in which they were carried out, the Rorschach tests were "almost useless by present standards; one might also mention a number of other invalidating factors, such as the dated technique by which the records were generated and the use of interpreters in some of the Rorschach interviews. Moreover, in some cases no verbatim notes were taken, whereas other protocols were recorded by a psychologist who seems to have had no previous experience with the Rorschach."</span></li>

Nuremberg Defendants at ukmc. Retrieved 21 November 2012.</span></li>

In the early morning hours of Wednesday, October 16, 1946, ten men died in the courthouse gymnasium in a botched hanging that left some strangled to death for as long as 25 minutes.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANuremberg+trials&amp;rft.atitle=Judgment+at+Nuremberg&amp;rft.aufirst=Robert&amp;rft.aulast=Shnayerson&amp;rft.date=1996-10&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Smithsonian+Magazine&amp;rft.pages=124-141&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fw3.salemstate.edu%2F~cmauriello%2Fpdf_his102%2Fnuremberg.pdf&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-All_Time.2C_part_two-72"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-All_Time.2C_part_two_72-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20090302002906/http://www.law.uga.edu/academics/profiles/dwilkes_more/his34_trial2.html">"The Trial of the Century– and of All Time, part two"</a>. <i>Flagpole Magazine</i>. 17 July 2002. p.&#160;6. John C. Woods, botched the execution. A number of the hanged Nazis died, not quickly from a broken neck as intended, but agonizingly from slow <a href="/wiki/Strangulation" class="mw-redirect" title="Strangulation">strangulation</a>. Ribbentrop and Sauckel each took 14 minutes to choke to death, while Keitel, whose death was the most painful, struggled for 28 minutes at the end of the rope before expiring.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANuremberg+trials&amp;rft.atitle=The+Trial+of+the+Century%E2%80%93+and+of+All+Time%2C+part+two&amp;rft.date=2002-07-17&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Flagpole+Magazine&amp;rft.pages=6&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.law.uga.edu%2Fdwilkes_more%2Fhis34_trial2.html&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-89"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-89">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFEhrenfreund2007" class="citation">Ehrenfreund, Norbert (2007), <i>The Nuremberg Legacy</i>, New York: Palgrave Macmillan</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANuremberg+trials&amp;rft.aufirst=Norbert&amp;rft.aulast=Ehrenfreund&amp;rft.btitle=The+Nuremberg+Legacy&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pub=Palgrave+Macmillan&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEFichtelberg20095-90"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFichtelberg20095_90-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFFichtelberg2009">Fichtelberg 2009</a>, p.&#160;5.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilbert199536-91"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilbert199536_91-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFGilbert1995">Gilbert 1995</a>, p.&#160;36.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEZolo2009-92"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZolo2009_92-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFZolo2009">Zolo 2009</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-lecturesarchive-93"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-lecturesarchive_93-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>See, e.g.,</i> statement of Professor Nicholls of St. Antony's College, Oxford, that "[t]he Nuremberg trials have not had a very good press. They are often depicted as a form of victor's justice in which people were tried for crimes which did not exist in law when they committed them, such as conspiring to start a war." <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.sant.ox.ac.uk/events/lecturesarchive/nicholls.html">Prof. Anthony Nicholls, University of Oxford</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiddiss1995-94"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiddiss1995_94-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBiddiss1995">Biddiss 1995</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-bbc-95"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-bbc_95-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>See, e.g.,</i> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.bbc.co.uk/history/worldwars/wwtwo/war_crimes_trials_01.shtml">BBC Article for BBC by Prof. Richard Overy</a> ("[T]hat the war crimes trials... were expressions of a legally dubious 'victors' justice' was [a point raised by]... senior [Allied] legal experts who doubted the legality of the whole process... There was no precedent. No other civilian government had ever been put on trial by the authorities of other states... What the Allied powers had in mind was a tribunal that would make the waging of aggressive war, the violation of sovereignty and the perpetration of what came to be known in 1945 as 'crimes against humanity' internationally recognized offences. Unfortunately, these had not previously been defined as crimes in international law, which left the Allies in the legally dubious position of having to execute retrospective justice&#160;– to punish actions that were not regarded as crimes at the time they were committed.")</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-allacademic-96"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-allacademic_96-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>See</i> Paper of Jonathan Graubart, San Diego State University, Political Science Department, published online <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.allacademic.com//meta/p_mla_apa_research_citation/2/5/1/1/4/pages251148/p251148-1.php">Graubart Article</a>, referring to the <i>ex post facto</i> nature of the charges.</span></li>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Avalon_Project">Avalon Project</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuremberg_trials&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Avalon Project">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>These citations refer to documents at <cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://avalon.law.yale.edu/subject_menus/imt.asp">"The International Military Tribunal for Germany"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Avalon_Project" title="Avalon Project">The Avalon Project: Documents in Law, History, and Diplomacy</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Yale_Law_School" title="Yale Law School">Yale Law School</a> <a href="/wiki/Lillian_Goldman_Law_Library" title="Lillian Goldman Law Library">Lillian Goldman Law Library</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANuremberg+trials&amp;rft.atitle=The+International+Military+Tribunal+for+Germany&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Avalon+Project%3A+Documents+in+Law%2C+History%2C+and+Diplomacy&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Favalon.law.yale.edu%2Fsubject_menus%2Fimt.asp&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></p>
